    Quick Reference & Cheat Sheets

    This is your quick reference guide for Kubernetes essentials. Use this as a cheat sheet during development, debugging, or interviews. Everything you need, organized for fast lookup.

    🚀 Essential kubectl Commands

    Cluster Information

    ▼ 
    # View cluster info
kubectl cluster-info

# Get cluster nodes
kubectl get nodes

# View node details
kubectl describe node <node-name>

# Check kubectl version
kubectl version --client

# View current context
kubectl config current-context

# View all contexts
kubectl config get-contexts

# Switch context
kubectl config use-context <context-name>

    Pod Operations

    ▼ 
    # List all pods
kubectl get pods

# List pods with more details
kubectl get pods -o wide

# List pods in all namespaces
kubectl get pods --all-namespaces

# View pod details
kubectl describe pod <pod-name>

# View pod logs
kubectl logs <pod-name>

# View logs with follow (tail -f)
kubectl logs -f <pod-name>

# View logs for a specific container
kubectl logs <pod-name> -c <container-name>

# Execute command in pod
kubectl exec -it <pod-name> -- /bin/bash

# Delete pod
kubectl delete pod <pod-name>

# Create pod from YAML
kubectl apply -f pod.yaml

    Deployment Operations

    ▼ 
    # List deployments
kubectl get deployments

# Create deployment
kubectl create deployment <name> --image=<image>

# Scale deployment
kubectl scale deployment <name> --replicas=3

# Update deployment image
kubectl set image deployment/<name> <container>=<image>

# View deployment status
kubectl rollout status deployment/<name>

# View rollout history
kubectl rollout history deployment/<name>

# Rollback deployment
kubectl rollout undo deployment/<name>

# Rollback to specific revision
kubectl rollout undo deployment/<name> --to-revision=2

    Service Operations

    ▼ 
    # List services
kubectl get services

# Expose deployment as service
kubectl expose deployment <name> --port=80 --type=NodePort

# Create service from YAML
kubectl apply -f service.yaml

# View service endpoints
kubectl get endpoints <service-name>

# Delete service
kubectl delete service <service-name>

    ConfigMap & Secrets

    ▼ 
    # List configmaps
kubectl get configmaps

# Create configmap from literal
kubectl create configmap <name> --from-literal=<key>=<value>

# Create configmap from file
kubectl create configmap <name> --from-file=<path>

# View configmap
kubectl describe configmap <name>

# List secrets
kubectl get secrets

# Create secret from literal
kubectl create secret generic <name> --from-literal=<key>=<value>

# Create secret from file
kubectl create secret generic <name> --from-file=<path>

# Decode secret value
kubectl get secret <name> -o jsonpath='{.data.<key>}' | base64 -d

    Debugging & Troubleshooting

    ▼ 
    # Get pod events
kubectl get events --sort-by=.metadata.creationTimestamp

# View pod YAML (applied configuration)
kubectl get pod <pod-name> -o yaml

# View pod logs (previous instance if crashed)
kubectl logs <pod-name> --previous

# Port forward to local port
kubectl port-forward <pod-name> 8080:80

# Copy files from pod
kubectl cp <pod-name>:/path/to/file ./local-file

# Copy files to pod
kubectl cp ./local-file <pod-name>:/path/to/file

# Check resource usage
kubectl top nodes
kubectl top pods

# View resource quotas
kubectl describe quota

    Namespace Management

    ▼ 
    # List namespaces
kubectl get namespaces

# Create namespace
kubectl create namespace <name>

# Set namespace for current context
kubectl config set-context --current --namespace=<name>

# Delete everything in namespace
kubectl delete all --all -n <namespace>

    Apply & Delete

    ▼ 
    # Apply from file
kubectl apply -f <file.yaml>

# Apply from directory
kubectl apply -f <directory>

# Delete from file
kubectl delete -f <file.yaml>

# Edit resource live
kubectl edit <resource-type> <name>

# Dry run (don't actually apply)
kubectl apply -f <file.yaml> --dry-run=client

# View diff
kubectl diff -f <file.yaml>

    📝 Common YAML Patterns

    Deployment Template

    ▼ 
    apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: Deployment
metadata:
  name: nginx-deployment
  labels:
    app: nginx
spec:
  replicas: 3
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: nginx
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        app: nginx
    spec:
      containers:
      - name: nginx
        image: nginx:1.21
        ports:
        - containerPort: 80
        resources:
          requests:
            memory: "64Mi"
            cpu: "250m"
          limits:
            memory: "128Mi"
            cpu: "500m"
        livenessProbe:
          httpGet:
            path: /
            port: 80
          initialDelaySeconds: 10
          periodSeconds: 5
        readinessProbe:
          httpGet:
            path: /
            port: 80
          initialDelaySeconds: 5
          periodSeconds: 5

    Service (ClusterIP)

    ▼ 
    apiVersion: v1
kind: Service
metadata:
  name: nginx-service
spec:
  type: ClusterIP
  selector:
    app: nginx
  ports:
  - protocol: TCP
    port: 80
    targetPort: 80

    Service (LoadBalancer)

    ▼ 
    apiVersion: v1
kind: Service
metadata:
  name: nginx-service
spec:
  type: LoadBalancer
  selector:
    app: nginx
  ports:
  - protocol: TCP
    port: 80
    targetPort: 80

    ConfigMap

    ▼ 
    apiVersion: v1
kind: ConfigMap
metadata:
  name: app-config
data:
  database_host: "postgres.default.svc.cluster.local"
  database_port: "5432"
  cache_enabled: "true"
  log_level: "info"
---
# Usage in Pod:
env:
- name: DATABASE_HOST
  valueFrom:
    configMapKeyRef:
      name: app-config
      key: database_host

    Secret

    ▼ 
    apiVersion: v1
kind: Secret
metadata:
  name: app-secret
type: Opaque
data:
  # Values must be base64 encoded
  # echo -n 'admin' | base64
  username: YWRtaW4=
  # echo -n 'password123' | base64
  password: cGFzc3dvcmQxMjM=
---
# Usage in Pod:
env:
- name: USERNAME
  valueFrom:
    secretKeyRef:
      name: app-secret
      key: username

    PersistentVolume & PersistentVolumeClaim

    ▼ 
    apiVersion: v1
kind: PersistentVolume
metadata:
  name: pv-volume
spec:
  capacity:
    storage: 10Gi
  accessModes:
    - ReadWriteOnce
  persistentVolumeReclaimPolicy: Retain
  hostPath:
    path: /mnt/data
---
apiVersion: v1
kind: PersistentVolumeClaim
metadata:
  name: pv-claim
spec:
  accessModes:
    - ReadWriteOnce
  resources:
    requests:
      storage: 5Gi
---
# Usage in Pod:
volumeMounts:
- name: storage
  mountPath: /data
volumes:
- name: storage
  persistentVolumeClaim:
    claimName: pv-claim

    Ingress

    ▼ 
    apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
kind: Ingress
metadata:
  name: nginx-ingress
  annotations:
    nginx.ingress.kubernetes.io/rewrite-target: /
spec:
  ingressClassName: nginx
  rules:
  - host: example.com
    http:
      paths:
      - path: /
        pathType: Prefix
        backend:
          service:
            name: nginx-service
            port:
              number: 80

    ⚡ Resource Limits Guidelines

    Understanding Requests vs Limits

    • Requests: Guaranteed resources. Kubernetes schedules based on this.
    • Limits: Maximum resources. Container throttled or killed if exceeded.
    • Best Practice: Always set both. Prevents resource starvation and noisy neighbors.

    Microservice Guidelines

    ▼
    Service Size CPU Request CPU Limit Memory Request Memory Limit
    Small (API, worker) 100m 500m 128Mi 256Mi
    Medium (web server) 250m 1000m 256Mi 512Mi
    Large (app server) 500m 2000m 512Mi 1Gi
    Extra Large (batch job) 1000m 4000m 1Gi 2Gi

    Note: 1000m = 1 CPU core. Adjust based on actual usage monitoring.

    CPU Units Explained

    ▼
    • 100m = 0.1 CPU = 10% of 1 core
    • 500m = 0.5 CPU = 50% of 1 core
    • 1000m = 1 CPU = 1 full core
    • 2000m = 2 CPU = 2 full cores

    Example: If you set cpu: 100m, your container is guaranteed 10% of a CPU core. If you set limits.cpu: 500m, it won't exceed 50% of a core.

    Memory Units Explained

    ▼
    • 128Mi = 128 Mebibytes ≈ 134 MB
    • 256Mi = 256 Mebibytes ≈ 268 MB
    • 1Gi = 1 Gibibyte ≈ 1.07 GB
    • 1G = 1 Gigabyte = 1 GB

    Gotcha: Kubernetes uses mebibytes (Mi) and gibibytes (Gi) by default (power of 2). Use M and G for megabytes/gigabytes (power of 10). The difference is ~5%.

    📊 Pod Phases & What They Mean

    ✅ Pending

    Pod accepted but not running. Being scheduled or pulling images.

    Common causes: Cluster lacks resources, image pull slow, scheduling constraints.

    🏃 Running

    Pod is running. At least one container is still running or starting.

    Normal state: Everything is working as expected.

    ✅ Succeeded

    All containers terminated successfully (job completed).

    Normal for jobs: Batch jobs, one-time tasks.

    ❌ Failed

    All containers terminated, at least one failed.

    Check: kubectl describe pod for exit codes and events.

    ❓ Unknown

    State couldn't be obtained (usually communication error).

    Check: Node health, network connectivity.

    Container States (Inside Pod)

    • Waiting: Container starting, pulling image, or running probes
    • Running: Container executing without issues
    • Terminated: Container exited (check exit code: 0 = success, non-zero = error)

    🔍 Event Types & Troubleshooting

    Common Event Types

    ▼
    Event Meaning Action
    Pulled Successfully pulled container image None, this is good
    PullFailed Failed to pull image Check image name, registry auth, network
    Scheduled Pod assigned to a node None, this is good
    FailedScheduling Couldn't find node for pod Check resource requests, taints, selectors
    FailedMount Volume mount failed Check PVC, storage class, volume exists
    Killing Pod being terminated Normal if deleting, otherwise check signals
    Unhealthy Readiness/liveness probe failed Check probe configuration, app health
    BackOff Container crashing, retrying with delay Check logs, fix container crash loop

    Troubleshooting Flowchart

    ▼
    1

    Pod not starting?

    kubectl get pods - Check if Pending or Running

    If Pending: Check events with kubectl describe pod

    If CrashLoopBackOff: Check logs with kubectl logs

    2

    Image pull errors?

    Check image name and tag in YAML

    Verify registry credentials: kubectl get secrets

    Test locally: docker pull <image>

    3

    Can't reach service?

    Check service exists: kubectl get svc

    Check endpoints: kubectl get endpoints <service>

    Verify pod labels match service selector

    Check network policies blocking traffic

    4

    High CPU/Memory usage?

    Check metrics: kubectl top pods

    Review resource limits vs requests

    Check if limits are too low (throttling) or too high (OOM)

    5

    Pod restarting repeatedly?

    Check previous logs: kubectl logs <pod> --previous

    Look for app crashes (unhandled exceptions)

    Check liveness probe configuration

    Verify resource limits (OOMKilled)

    Common Exit Codes

    ▼
    Exit Code Meaning Troubleshoot
    0 Success Normal exit (job completed)
    1 General error Check application logs
    125 Container failed to run Docker daemon error, check image
    126 Command not executable Check entrypoint/command permissions
    127 Command not found Check command path in container
    137 SIGKILL (killed) OOMKilled, or manual kill
    139 SIGSEGV (segmentation fault) Application bug, memory issue
    143 SIGTERM (terminated) Graceful shutdown request

    🚩 Common kubectl Flags & Options

    Output Formats

    ▼ 
    # Table format (default)
kubectl get pods -o wide

# JSON format
kubectl get pod <name> -o json

# YAML format
kubectl get pod <name> -o yaml

# Custom columns
kubectl get pods -o custom-columns=NAME:.metadata.name,IP:.status.podIP

# JSONPath (extract specific fields)
kubectl get pod <name> -o jsonpath='{.status.podIP}'

# Show labels
kubectl get pods --show-labels

    Namespace & Context

    ▼ 
    # Specify namespace
kubectl get pods -n <namespace>
kubectl get pods --namespace=<namespace>

# All namespaces
kubectl get pods --all-namespaces
kubectl get pods -A

# Specify context
kubectl get pods --context=<context-name>

# Specify kubeconfig file
kubectl get pods --kubeconfig=<file>

    Selectors & Filters

    ▼ 
    # By label
kubectl get pods -l app=nginx
kubectl get pods -l 'env in (dev,prod)'

# By field selector
kubectl get pods --field-selector=status.phase=Running

# Watch for changes (stream)
kubectl get pods -w

# Show resource usage
kubectl top pods --containers

# Sort by field
kubectl get pods --sort-by=.metadata.creationTimestamp

    Useful Utility Flags

    ▼ 
    # Dry run (don't execute)
kubectl apply -f file.yaml --dry-run=client

# Validate YAML (dry-run server)
kubectl apply -f file.yaml --dry-run=server

# Show diff
kubectl diff -f file.yaml

# Edit last applied configuration
kubectl edit deploy <name> --edit-last-applied

# Force delete (immediate)
kubectl delete pod <name> --force --grace-period=0

# Wait for condition
kubectl wait --for=condition=ready pod/<name>

# Verbosity (for debugging kubectl itself)
kubectl get pods -v=9

    ⚡ Performance Tuning Parameters

    Resource Optimization

    ▼

    CPU Optimization

    • Set appropriate requests: Base on actual usage, not guesses
    • Use CPU limits wisely: Too low = throttling, too high = noisy neighbor
    • Enable horizontal autoscaling: HPA adds pods when CPU high
    • Avoid CPU overcommit: Sum of requests ≤ node capacity

    Memory Optimization

    • Set limits higher than requests: Prevents OOM, allows bursts
    • Monitor memory leaks: If memory grows constantly, fix app
    • Use vertical autoscaling: VPA adjusts based on usage
    • Profile with tools: Use heap profilers to find leaks

    Probe Tuning

    ▼

    Liveness Probe Guidelines

    • initialDelaySeconds: Give app time to start (10-30s)
    • periodSeconds: Check frequency (10-30s)
    • timeoutSeconds: Response timeout (1-5s)
    • failureThreshold: Failures before restart (3-5)
    • Gotcha: Too aggressive = restart loops. Too lenient = dead pods stay running.

    Readiness Probe Guidelines

    • Faster than liveness: Smaller delays, more frequent checks
    • Check dependencies: Database, cache, external services
    • Grace period: Allow for connection establishment
    • Gotcha: If ready check fails, traffic stops but pod stays running.

    Autoscaling Parameters

    ▼

    Horizontal Pod Autoscaler (HPA)

    apiVersion: autoscaling/v2
kind: HorizontalPodAutoscaler
metadata:
  name: app-hpa
spec:
  scaleTargetRef:
    apiVersion: apps/v1
    kind: Deployment
    name: app
  minReplicas: 2
  maxReplicas: 10
  metrics:
  - type: Resource
    resource:
      name: cpu
      target:
        type: Utilization
        averageUtilization: 70

    Guidelines:

    • Target 70% CPU for scaling (allows headroom)
    • Set min/max replicas based on load patterns
    • Use custom metrics for autoscaling on queue length, RPS

    📊 Monitoring Metrics to Watch

    Cluster-Level Metrics

    • Node health: % Ready, CPU/Memory usage
    • Pod distribution: Even spread across nodes?
    • Resource utilization: Cluster CPU/Memory %
    • API server latency: Control plane responsiveness

    Pod-Level Metrics

    • Restart count: Frequent restarts = crash loop
    • CPU throttling: At limits often?
    • Memory usage: Near limit = OOM risk
    • Network I/O: Traffic patterns

    Application Metrics

    • Request rate: RPS per pod
    • Error rate: 5xx errors increasing?
    • Latency: P95, P99 response times
    • Queue depth: Worker queue size

    Business Metrics

    • SLI/SLO: Error budget, uptime
    • Deployment frequency: How often you ship
    • Lead time: Deploy to production
    • MTTR: Mean time to recovery

    Alerting Thresholds (Starting Points)

    Metric Warning Critical
    Pod Restart Rate > 1/hour > 5/hour
    CPU Usage 70% 90%
    Memory Usage 80% 95%
    Disk Usage 70% 85%
    API Server Latency 500ms 1s
    Error Rate 1% 5%

    ⚠️ Common Gotchas & Pitfalls

    "My pods can't reach each other"

    Critical

    The gotcha: Pods CAN reach each other by IP, but you should use Services.

    Why: Pod IPs change when pods restart. Services provide stable DNS names.

    Fix:

    • Create a Service for each pod you need to reach
    • Use <service-name>.<namespace>.svc.cluster.local
    • In same namespace, just <service-name> works
    # Good: Use service DNS
http://my-service.default.svc.cluster.local

# Bad: Use pod IP (changes on restart)
http://10.244.1.5

    "Pod stuck in Pending state"

    High

    The gotcha: You requested resources the cluster doesn't have.

    Common causes:

    • CPU/Memory requests exceed node capacity
    • No nodes match your node selector
    • Tolerations missing for tainted nodes
    • PVC can't find matching PV

    Fix:

    # Check what's wrong
kubectl describe pod <pod-name>

# Common output:
# 0/3 nodes are available: 3 Insufficient cpu

# Fix: Lower your requests or add nodes

    "CrashLoopBackOff - what does that mean?"

    High

    The gotcha: Your container is starting, then crashing, repeatedly.

    Kubernetes is trying to help: It restarts the container with increasing delays (backoff).

    Common causes:

    • Application error (exception on startup)
    • Misconfigured environment variables
    • Missing ConfigMaps/Secrets
    • Wrong command/entrypoint

    Fix:

    # Check the logs
kubectl logs <pod-name>

# If previous crashed, check previous logs
kubectl logs <pod-name> --previous

    "OOMKilled - but I have enough memory!"

    High

    The gotcha: You set memory limit too low, or not at all.

    Without limits: Container can use all node memory, then gets killed.

    With low limits: Container hits limit and gets killed.

    Fix:

    • Always set memory limits
    • Monitor actual usage with kubectl top pods
    • Set limits 20-30% above observed peak
    • If growing continuously, fix memory leak in app
    resources:
  requests:
    memory: "128Mi"    # Guaranteed minimum
  limits:
    memory: "256Mi"    # Maximum before OOMKill

    "Image pull errors in production"

    Medium

    The gotcha: Private registry authentication not configured.

    What's happening: Pod can't pull image because it needs credentials.

    Fix: Create image pull secret

    # Create secret from Docker config
kubectl create secret docker-registry regcred \
  --docker-server=<registry-url> \
  --docker-username=<user> \
  --docker-password=<password>

# Use in pod spec
spec:
  imagePullSecrets:
  - name: regcred

    "My pods are on the wrong nodes"

    Medium

    The gotcha: Scheduler places pods randomly unless you guide it.

    Fix options:

    • Node selectors: Simple label matching
    • Affinity: Advanced rules (co-locate, spread)
    • Taints/tolerations: Dedicated nodes
    # Node selector (simple)
spec:
  nodeSelector:
    disktype: ssd

# Affinity (advanced)
spec:
  affinity:
    podAntiAffinity:
      requiredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
      - labelSelector:
          matchExpressions:
          - key: app
            operator: In
            values:
            - nginx
        topologyKey: kubernetes.io/hostname

    "Rolling update broke everything"

    Critical

    The gotcha: New version deployed but failing, and now you're down.

    Immediate fix: Rollback

    # Rollback to previous version
kubectl rollout undo deployment/<name>

# Check rollout history
kubectl rollout history deployment/<name>

# Rollback to specific revision
kubectl rollout undo deployment/<name> --to-revision=2

    Prevention:

    • Use readiness probes (don't route traffic to unready pods)
    • Set maxSurge and maxUnavailable carefully
    • Consider blue-green deployments for critical apps

    "Config changes not applying"

    Medium

    The gotcha: ConfigMaps/Secrets don't auto-trigger pod restarts.

    What's happening: You updated ConfigMap, but pods still have old env vars.

    Fix options:

    • Restart pods: kubectl rollout restart deployment/<name>
    • Use checksum annotation to force rollout
    • Mount ConfigMap as volume (updates in real-time)
    # Force restart when config changes
kind: Deployment
spec:
  template:
    metadata:
      annotations:
        checksum/config: "{{ include (print $.Template.BasePath \"/configmap.yaml\") . | sha256sum }}"

    🎯 Decision Flowcharts

    Which Controller to Use?

    ▼

    Quick Decision Guide

    • Deployment: Stateful apps, web servers, APIs (95% of cases)
    • StatefulSet: Databases, distributed systems requiring stable identities
    • DaemonSet: Logging agents, monitoring on every node
    • Job: One-time tasks, batch processing
    • CronJob: Scheduled tasks, periodic backups
    Use Case Controller Why
    Web server, API, microservice Deployment Easy scaling, rolling updates
    Database (PostgreSQL, MySQL) StatefulSet Stable network, storage identity
    Logging agent (Fluentd) DaemonSet Run on every node automatically
    One-time data migration Job Run to completion, track success
    Daily backup job CronJob Scheduled, repeated execution
    Message queue worker Deployment Scale based on queue length
    Redis cluster StatefulSet Stable IPs, stable storage

    Which Service Type?

    ▼
    Service Type Use When Scope
    ClusterIP Internal service-to-service communication Cluster internal only
    NodePort Expose externally temporarily (testing/dev) External via <NodeIP>:<Port>
    LoadBalancer Expose to internet (cloud provider LB) External via cloud LB IP
    ExternalName Map to external DNS name (alias) DNS CNAME

    Best Practice

    Most common pattern:

    1. Use ClusterIP for internal services (default)
    2. Use Ingress for external HTTP/HTTPS traffic
    3. Use LoadBalancer for external non-HTTP traffic (rare)

    Should I Use Kubernetes?

    ▼

    ✅ Yes, use Kubernetes when:

    • You're running 10+ containers across multiple servers
    • You need high availability and self-healing
    • You have complex microservices architecture
    • You're doing multi-cloud or hybrid deployments
    • You have DevOps expertise or can hire it
    • You need to scale applications automatically

    ❌ No, don't use Kubernetes when:

    • You have a simple monolith on one server
    • You don't have DevOps resources to maintain it
    • You're just starting with containers (learn Docker first)
    • You need something running today without complexity
    • You're okay with managed services (Lambda, App Engine)
    • Your team is small and product-focused, not infra-focused

    🏗️ Architecture Diagram Templates

    Standard Three-Tier Architecture

    Typical web application architecture with frontend, API, workers, and data layer.

    Microservices Architecture Pattern

    Microservices with service mesh for traffic management, security, and observability.

    💡 Quick Tips for Success

    Start Small

    Don't deploy your entire architecture on day one. Start with one service, learn the patterns, then expand.

    Use Namespaces

    Separate environments (dev/staging/prod) with namespaces. Prevents accidental deletions and resource conflicts.

    Label Everything

    Add labels to every resource. Makes querying, organizing, and debugging 10x easier.

    Set Resource Limits

    Always set requests and limits. Prevents noisy neighbors and OOMKilled pods.

    Monitor Everything

    Set up Prometheus + Grafana from day one. You can't improve what you don't measure.

    Use GitOps

    Store all YAML in git. Use ArgoCD or Flux for deployments. Every change is tracked and reversible.

    Practice Rollbacks

    Know how to rollback before you deploy. Test it in non-production first.

    Learn YAML Structure

    Understand the schema: apiVersion → kind → metadata → spec. Makes every manifest make sense.

    🎯 Key Takeaway

    This reference guide is your quick lookup companion throughout your Kubernetes journey. Bookmark it, keep it open while you work, and refer to it when you're stuck.

    The essentials to remember:

    • kubectl get/describe/logs - Your debugging trifecta
    • Always set resource limits - Prevents OOM and resource starvation
    • Use Services, not Pod IPs - Stable networking
    • Check events first - Tells you what Kubernetes is trying to do
    • Readiness > Liveness - Prevent traffic to unready pods

    Remember: This is a reference, not a tutorial. Learn the concepts in the other sections, then use this guide to look up specifics.

    The Kubernetes Ecosystem

    Beyond the Cluster

    Kubernetes doesn't exist in isolation. To run K8s in production, you need an entire ecosystem of tools and technologies that surround and integrate with it.

    🌐 Why the Ecosystem Matters

    Kubernetes is powerful, but it's not a complete solution. Think of K8s as an operating system for the data center. Just as you need applications, tools, and utilities around an OS, you need ecosystem tools around K8s.

    Production reality: I've never seen a production K8s deployment that was "just Kubernetes." Every successful deployment includes monitoring, logging, CI/CD, secret management, and more.

    Ecosystem Categories

    🐳 Container Runtime

    What actually runs your containers

    Docker
    Foundational
    Connection to K8s: Original container runtime. K8s used to depend directly on Docker.

    What it does: Builds, ships, and runs containers.

    Status: K8s moved away from Docker as direct runtime. Now uses CRI-O or containerd directly.

    When you need it: Building container images. Docker still standard for development.

    Production reality: We use Docker for building images, but our clusters use containerd as the runtime. The industry shifted: Docker → containerd/CRI-O.

    containerd
    Industry Standard
    Connection to K8s: Container runtime that K8s uses directly (via CRI).

    What it does: Manages container lifecycle (pull, create, start, stop, delete).

    Status: Default runtime for most K8s distributions. Graduated from CNCF.

    When you need it: Running K8s in production. Already included in most managed K8s offerings.

    CRI-O
    Alternative
    Connection to K8s: Lightweight container runtime alternative to containerd.

    What it does: Implements Kubernetes Container Runtime Interface (CRI). Optimized for K8s.

    Status: Popular in distros like OpenShift, OKD.

    📦 Package Management

    Managing complex applications

    Helm
    Essential
    Connection to K8s: Package manager. Installs and manages K8s applications.

    What it does: Installs entire applications (database + cache + web app + config) with one command.

    Status: De facto standard for K8s package management. Version 3 is current major version.

    When you need it: Deploying complex apps with multiple resources. Managing third-party applications.

    Production reality: We deploy everything via Helm charts. Makes upgrades, rollbacks, and environment changes manageable. One chart, multiple environments (dev/staging/prod).

    🔗 Service Mesh

    Managing service-to-service communication

    Istio
    Popular
    Connection to K8s: Service mesh that sits on top of K8s. Intercepts all pod-to-pod traffic.

    What it does: Traffic management, security (mTLS), observability (tracing, metrics), resilience (retries, circuit breakers).

    Status: Most feature-rich service mesh. Complex but powerful.

    When you need it: Microservices with complex communication patterns. Need mTLS between services.

    Production reality: We adopted Istio for microservices. The observability (tracing every request between services) was game-changing for debugging. But complexity is real — requires dedicated expertise.

    Linkerd
    Simpler Alternative
    Connection to K8s: Lightweight service mesh. Easier to operate than Istio.

    What it does: Core service mesh features (mTLS, observability, reliability) with simpler setup.

    Status: Simpler than Istio, fewer features. Good for teams starting with service mesh.

    📊 Monitoring & Observability

    Understanding what's happening in your cluster

    Prometheus
    Essential
    Connection to K8s: Scrapes metrics from K8s API and applications. Time-series database.

    What it does: Collects metrics, stores them, provides query language (PromQL). Alerts on conditions.

    Status: CNCF graduated project. De facto standard for K8s monitoring.

    When you need it: ANY K8s deployment. You need metrics. Period.

    Production reality: Prometheus + Grafana is standard K8s monitoring stack. We have 50+ Prometheus instances, each scraping a subset of services. Central Grafana for dashboards.

    Grafana
    Essential
    Connection to K8s: Visualization layer for Prometheus (and other data sources).

    What it does: Dashboards, graphs, alerts. Makes metrics understandable and actionable.

    Status: Universal visualization. Works with Prometheus, Loki, Elasticsearch, etc.

    ELK Stack / Loki
    Important
    Connection to K8s: Centralized logging. Collect logs from all pods.

    What it does: Aggregates logs from all containers. Searchable, queryable.

    Status: Loki is CNCF project (Grafana ecosystem). ELK stack (Elasticsearch, Logstash, Kibana) is traditional alternative.

    🚀 CI/CD & GitOps

    Deploying to Kubernetes automatically

    ArgoCD
    GitOps Standard
    Connection to K8s: GitOps operator. Ensures cluster state matches git repo.

    What it does: Monitors git repo. Automatically syncs changes to K8s cluster. Provides rollback.

    Status: Popular GitOps tool. CNCF incubating project.

    When you need it: Automating deployments to K8s. GitOps workflow.

    Production reality: We moved from manual kubectl apply to ArgoCD. Changes go through PR → review → merge → ArgoCD deploys. Audit trail + rollback + consistency. Night and day difference.

    Flux
    Alternative
    Connection to K8s: GitOps toolkit from Weaveworks. Similar to ArgoCD.

    What it does: GitOps continuous delivery. Syncs git state to K8s.

    Status: CNCF incubating project. Lighter weight than ArgoCD.

    Tekton
    CI Framework
    Connection to K8s: K8s-native CI framework. Runs CI pipelines in K8s pods.

    What it does: Defines and runs CI/CD pipelines as K8s resources.

    🚪 Ingress & Traffic Management

    Getting traffic into your cluster

    NGINX Ingress Controller
    Popular
    Connection to K8s: Implements K8s Ingress resource. Routes HTTP/HTTPS traffic.

    What it does: Load balancing, SSL termination, URL-based routing, WebSocket support.

    Status: Most popular ingress controller. Battle-tested.

    When you need it: Exposing HTTP/HTTPS services to external traffic.

    Production reality: We use NGINX Ingress for all HTTP services. Handles SSL termination, path-based routing, and canary deployments. Essential.

    Traefik
    Modern Alternative
    Connection to K8s: Modern ingress controller with automatic service discovery.

    What it does: Ingress, load balancing, service discovery. Dynamic configuration.

    HAProxy
    High Performance
    Connection to K8s: High-performance ingress/controller via HAProxy ingress.

    What it does: Enterprise-grade load balancing. Advanced features.

    💾 Storage & Databases

    Persistent storage for stateful workloads

    Longhorn
    Storage Platform
    Connection to K8s: Distributed block storage built specifically for K8s.

    What it does: Provides persistent storage for stateful apps. Backups, snapshots, disaster recovery.

    Status: CNCF sandbox project. Easy to get started.

    When you need it: Running databases or other stateful workloads in K8s.

    Rook
    Storage Orchestrator
    Connection to K8s: Orchestrates Ceph (storage system) on K8s.

    What it does: Turns storage software (Ceph) into K8s resources.

    Cloud Provider Storage
    Managed Options
    Connection to K8s: AWS EBS, GCE PD, Azure Disk. Integrated via CSI drivers.

    What it does: Cloud-native persistent storage. Easy to use, tied to cloud provider.

    🔐 Security & Policy

    Securing your cluster and applications

    OPA Gatekeeper
    Policy Engine
    Connection to K8s: Admission controller. Enforces policies before objects created.

    What it does: Validates configurations against policies. "All containers must run as non-root", "Images must come from approved registry."

    Status: CNCF graduating project.

    When you need it: Governance, compliance, preventing misconfigurations.

    Production reality: We use Gatekeeper to enforce standards. Prevents developers from creating pods without resource limits, or exposing services as LoadBalancer in dev. Saves us from bad configurations.

    Vault
    Secret Management
    Connection to K8s: External secret management. Injects secrets into pods at runtime.

    What it does: Secure secret storage. Dynamic secrets, encryption, audit logging.

    Status: Industry standard for secret management. Better than K8s secrets (which are just base64).

    When to Use What: Decision Tree

    🌳 Ecosystem Decision Framework

    Question 1: What's your main need?

    Deploying complex apps?

    → Use Helm. It's the standard for a reason.

    Monitoring?

    → Use Prometheus + Grafana. No question.

    Service-to-service security?

    → Use Istio (or Linkerd for simpler needs).

    Automated deployments?

    → Use ArgoCD for GitOps.

    Question 2: How big is your team?

    Small team (<5 engineers)?

    → Stick to basics. Prometheus, Grafana, Helm. Add service mesh when microservices get complex.

    Medium team (5-20 engineers)?

    → Standard stack: Prometheus + Grafana + Helm + ArgoCD + service mesh.

    Large team (20+ engineers)?

    → Full ecosystem with custom tooling. Consider multiple service meshes, multi-cluster management.

    Question 3: What's your cloud provider?

    AWS?

    → EKS + EBS + ALB. Consider AWS Load Balancer Controller.

    GCP?

    → GKE + Cloud Storage + Cloud Armor. Integrated GCP ecosystem.

    Azure?

    → AKS + Azure Disk + Azure Gateway. Integrated Azure ecosystem.

    On-prem / Multi-cloud?

    → Vanilla K8s + Portworks / Rancher. Avoid cloud-specific lock-in.

    How Everything Connects

    🔗 The Complete Production Stack

    Layer 1: Infrastructure

    Cloud (AWS/GCP/Azure) or On-prem servers → Kubernetes (via kubeadm, EKS, GKE, AKS)

    Layer 2: Container Runtime

    containerd or CRI-O (runs the containers)

    Layer 3: Kubernetes

    Deployments, Services, Ingress (your application resources)

    Layer 4: Package Management

    Helm charts (bundle all resources together)

    Layer 5: Networking

    Service Mesh (Istio/Linkerd) → Ingress Controller (NGINX) → External Traffic

    Layer 6: Observability

    Prometheus (metrics) + Grafana (dashboards) + Loki/ELK (logs)

    Layer 7: CI/CD

    Git (source of truth) → ArgoCD/Flux (automatic deployment)

    Layer 8: Security

    Vault (secrets) + Gatekeeper (policies) + RBAC (access control)

    Key insight: Kubernetes sits in the middle, but needs all these layers to be production-ready. You can start simple, but you'll end up with most of these.

    What's Next

    🎯 Focus on the Basics First

    Master K8s fundamentals before diving into the ecosystem. Learn pods, deployments, services first.

    📊 Monitoring is Non-Negotiable

    Every production K8s deployment needs Prometheus + Grafana. Do it first.

    📦 Use Helm Early

    Don't manage resources manually. Use Helm charts from day one.

    🔐 Security from the Start

    Implement RBAC, network policies, and secret management before production.

    Ecosystem Quick Reference

    📋 Essential Tools (Must-Have)

    • containerd - Container runtime (built-in to most K8s)
    • Helm - Package management (use it for everything)
    • Prometheus - Metrics collection (non-negotiable)
    • Grafana - Dashboards and visualization
    • NGINX Ingress - Traffic routing (or Traefik)

    🚀 Production-Ready Tools (Should-Have)

    • ArgoCD - GitOps deployments
    • Istio - Service mesh (for microservices)
    • Loki - Log aggregation
    • Vault - Secret management
    • Longhorn - Storage (for stateful apps)

    🔧 Nice-to-Have Tools

    • Flux - Alternative to ArgoCD
    • Linkerd - Lighter service mesh
    • OPA Gatekeeper - Policy enforcement
    • Tekton - K8s-native CI
    • Kustomize - Configuration management

    🎯 Key Takeaway

    Kubernetes is powerful but incomplete. The ecosystem makes it production-ready.

    Start with: containerd (runtime) + Helm (packages) + Prometheus/Grafana (monitoring) + NGINX Ingress (traffic).

    Add as needed: Service mesh for microservices, ArgoCD for GitOps, Vault for secrets, Loki for logs.

    Production reality: Every successful K8s deployment uses most of these tools. The ecosystem isn't optional — it's how K8s works in the real world.
